B003P2WO5E|3.0|This book could have been so good. You follow a few characters, characters you can really root for. Characters you really want o get involved in their story, however the author refuses to let this happen.<br />I am a fantasy book lover; I am familiar with the idea of switching from one character to another between chapter sets. The issue with this book comes with how often he switches. Every single chapter either changes which character you’re following or the time in which you were following the character. This might not sound so bad but it never lets you really get drawn into any specific character, it never lets you really become emotionally involved with the characters.<br /><br />The other problem with this book is there is no driving force. I remember being 77% of the way done this book and thinking to myself. “Who are the bad guys &#34;, &#34;what's the point in this story&#34;. Obviously each character has their own struggles in this book, but there is nothing that ties any two characters together. Sanderson references an evil force several times, they hint it might come back and be a main antagonist. Several times I told friends I’m not picking this book up ever again because there is no driving force. It follows three people, talks about their far from ordinary lives, but to what point? If you get free of this mess, then what? Is the story over? If all three of you died, the world would be fine, your life would be over which I suppose would suck, on the flip side This is supposed to be a 10 book series, so i understand a slow start, but this is something else. Shouldn’t you want your readers to want to read the book because it draws them in, not because they started it and refuse to give up?<br />The whole time I read, I was thinking to myself &#34;it’s going to get better, just wait&#34; mentally begging Sanderson to just once let me read to consecutive chapters about the same character. Let me get a decent way into one story before you change it on me again, but he never does. He refuses to let up. If anyone reads this book and does not feel this same frustration I will be shocked. Once again, I understand multiple story lines, but let one develop a bit before you cut to another. Its not the same suspense of not being able to wait to read about the other person again, its the annoyance of wanting to skip ahead a few chapters so you can keep your mind on one character for more than a few pages.<br /><br />At any point in the book, one of the three main characters could be killed and it wouldn’t make a difference in any way shape or form.<br /><br />In case Brandon Sanderson somehow reads this... I really like what you did with the Wheel of time series it was amazing. Your Mistborn trilogy was good, I read all three books and was very satisfied. I dont understand how you expected people to be drawn into this story when you dont actually let people read for long enough to become attached. PLEASE this is a cool world, the characters are good, just let me actually read one without a constant frustration. Let it build anticipation instead of frustration.<br />All this being said, I didn’t hate the novel, I did finish the book after all, but I can say I would NOT recommend this book.

B00BD99JMW|2.0|*** REVIEW CONTAINS SPOILERS ***<br /><br />Before I begin, I think it's important that I kind of let you know where I stand with the Divergent series up to this point. I LOVED Divergent so much. It pulled me out of a massive reading rut and made me fall in love with YA again. Then I read Insurgent and was not wowed. Honestly, I think I should have given the book more like 2.5 or 3 stars (I gave it 4.. no idea why, especially after re-reading my review).<br /><br />I was excited about Allegiant because of the big bang ending that was in Insurgent, but honestly my hopes weren't that high. I wasn't convinced that Allegiant was going to wow me, and I was right.<br /><br />***<br /><br />I don't like how Tris and Tobias pushed aside their problems by making out<br /><br />Tris and Tobias's relationship still isn't perfect in Allegiant. They have things they need to talk about and discuss. But ANY time this situation comes up, they bury it by making out. It's literally like:<br /><br />\\"We need to ta--\\"<br />Suddenly I felt his mouth on mine. [Cue make out scene.]<br /><br />Paraphrasing, but that's the gist of it. SO.MANY.TIMES. It's almost like they thought they had to make up for their fighting/distance in Insurgent, but this was not the right way to do it! You can't just hope your problems will go away by cutting them off with a kiss. It doesn't work like that.<br /><br />***<br /><br />I kind of wish Tris and Tobias just didn't have a relationship at all<br /><br />I grew to hate Tris and Tobias's relationship. Adding onto the previous point, I just felt like their relationship was so half ass. Except when they were making out, they didn't act like a couple. They didn't treat each other like equals. There were still plenty of times when they didn't clue each other in on their thoughts/plans. They just didn't feel TOGETHER.<br /><br />Maybe this is extremely stereotypical of me, but there were so many moments when I felt that Tris and Tobias should have reacted in a certain way as a couple (like comforted the other person, or sought comfort from them), but they reacted so indifferently instead.<br /><br />\\"I could stay and try to comfort him, but I need answers about my mother, and I'm not going to wait any longer.\\"<br /><br />Except when their lips were glued together, I just didn't look at them and think, \\"You guys are dating. You love each other.\\" Instead I saw.. colleagues? Maybe friends?<br /><br />I felt like labeling what they had as a relationship just brought the story down. I honestly would have preferred if their relationship was just cut from the book because it kept disappointing me.<br /><br />\\"But no more lies. Not ever\\"<br />\\"Okay.\\"<br />I feel stiff and squeezed, like my body was just forced into something too small for it.<br /><br />Because being completely open and honest with Tobias makes her feel restricted and contained? *eye roll*<br /><br />***<br /><br />Tobias and Tris had the same voice.<br /><br />In Allegiant, the chapters switch between Tris and Tobias's points of view, but I couldn't tell them apart. I often had to double check the chapter heading because I couldn't tell whose point of view I was reading from.<br /><br />I think if you're going to have alternating points of view, you have to have two very distinct voices. Tris and Tobias didn't sound or feel particularly different.<br /><br />***<br /><br />I didn't love any of the characters.<br /><br />Overall, the character development and relationships were poor. I already talked about Tris and Tobias, but I couldn't connect to ANY of the characters really. I just felt so little for them.<br /><br />Tobias on his own was weak. I remember loving him in Divergent because he was such a hardened badass. In Allegiant he's constantly doubting himself, wallowing in grief, blaming himself for everything... it got to be a bit pathetic and I just wanted to yell at him and tell him to quit with the self pity.<br /><br />I loved Christina in Divergent, but I was indifferent towards her in Allegiant.<br /><br />There were a bunch of other characters but they all felt so fleeting. They dodged in and out of the book and I never got a sense of who they were and never formed any attachments to them.<br /><br />***<br /><br />I hated how we were given two separate societies and two separate rebellions.<br /><br />It made it hard for me to figure out where I should concentrate. On the one hand we had the rebellion within the Chicago city. The factionless rebelled and are trying to take over and convert the whole city into a factionless society.<br /><br />Then on the other hand, we have the whole of the United States. With this society we have a whole other rebellion. Once this one started up, I felt like I was being pulled in two different directions. I didn't know if the rebellion in Chicago was still important, and I didn't know what to even focus on! I hated how we were introduced into one conflict, and then suddenly jerked into a different one.<br /><br />***<br /><br />The time period was weird.<br /><br />In Allegiant we get the sense that the story takes place at least hundreds of years in the future. We know that the experiments have been running for at least ~7 generations (about 210 years). But I don't think that when they started was 2013 either. It was probably at least 2050 when they started (if not later), then that means Allegiant takes place around 2260.<br /><br />The exact timing isn't important, but what is important is that the Divergent series takes place hundreds of years in the future. And yet... I thought there was so little advancement. All the tech seemed like 2013 tech. It's like advancement was stunted, but the years kept rolling by. They still use guns, normal trucks, airplane travel seems the same, etc. With all the wars and uprisings, I would have expected really advanced weaponry.<br /><br />Sure they had the serums and the fear landscapes, but that's about it...<br /><br />***<br /><br />I didn't like how the serums felt more like magic than science.<br /><br />In particular, I'm talking about the death serum. Surely this serum is like a chemical weapon, right? Like I imagine it being comparable to nerve gas. Everyone who comes into contact with it dies. And yet... Tris can magically survive it through mind power It's literally like she just willed herself not to die. I can understand overcoming fears and simulations, kind of like lucid dreaming. But I can't imagine the death serum being anything but a biological killer.. like nerve gas. And yet Tris can overcome it with just the power of her mind? O_O This makes zero sense to me.<br /><br />***<br /><br />I did not like the ending.<br /><br />Maybe some people will like it for being `powerful' or `gutsy' or `emotional'. I just didn't like it.<br /><br />[I'll be honest, I tend to hate any ending where the main character dies. It's just not how I like to read stories. And in Allegiant, Tris dies. Whenever this happens in books, I get left with a sense of \\"What's the point?\\" I spend three books reading from a character's point of view, watching them grow and change, connecting with them (hopefully), just to have them offed. Even if the conflict was resolved (in this case it was), I can't help but feeling like it was pointless or wasted time.<br /><br />But although I didn't like the ending, it wasn't because I loved Tris and couldn't bear to see her die. Emotionally, I didn't actually feel anything when she died. I wasn't upset or teary. I was only angry because of that whole `pointless' feeling. I think that just goes to show how detached I've become since Divergent.<br /><br />Additionally, I thought the ending with Tobias and his mother was so... lame... and easy. Tobias gave his mother an ultimatum. He said: stop trying to rule the city and form a treaty with the Allegiant, or lose me as your son. And she just pulled him into her arms and tearfully said, \\"I choose you!\\" Why couldn't he have saidthat to her an entire book ago?<br /><br />Also I felt nothing when Uriah died. Honestly I kind of forgot who he was going into Allegiant and he was so absent through the entire story that I just didn't care at all when he went into a coma, and I again didn't care at all when they pulled the plug on him.<br /><br />***<br /><br />Allegiant was about what I expected.<br /><br />After Insurgent, I think Allegiant was exactly what I expected it to be. I don't feel like Tris and Tobias's relationship ever \\"healed\\", and for the rest I was just mildly indifferent. Throw in a few other problems, and it's about what I expected it to be.<br /><br />I still look back on Divergent fondly, but I think overall I just don't like where the series went. I hated how it destroyed Tris and Tobias's relationship, I didn't love where the plot went, and I hated the ending Roth chose.I didn't like how the serums felt more like magic than science.<br /><br />In particular, I'm talking about the death serum. Surely this serum is like a chemical weapon, right? Like I imagine it being comparable to nerve gas. Everyone who comes into contact with it dies. And yet... Tris can magically survive it through mind power It's literally like she just willed herself not to die. I can understand overcoming fears and simulations, kind of like lucid dreaming. But I can't imagine the death serum being anything but a biological killer.. like nerve gas. And yet Tris can overcome it with just the power of her mind? I just didn't like it.<br /><br />[I'll be honest, I tend to hate any ending where the main character dies. It's just not how I like to read stories. And in Allegiant, Tris dies. Whenever this happens in books, I get left with a sense of \\"What's the point?\\" I spend three books reading from a character's point of view, watching them grow and change, connecting with them (hopefully), just to have them offed. Even if the conflict was resolved (in this case it was), I can't help but feeling like it was pointless or wasted time.<br /><br />But although I didn't like the ending, it wasn't because I loved Tris and couldn't bear to see her die. Emotionally, I didn't actually feel anything when she died. I wasn't upset or teary. I was only angry because of that whole `pointless' feeling. I think that just goes to show how detached I've become since Divergent.<br /><br />Additionally, I thought the ending with Tobias and his mother was so... lame... and easy. Tobias gave his mother an ultimatum. He said: stop trying to rule the city and form a treaty with the Allegiant, or lose me as your son. And she just pulled him into her arms and tearfully said, \\"I choose you!\\" Why couldn't he have saidthat to her an entire book ago?<br /><br />Also I felt nothing when Uriah died. Honestly I kind of forgot who he was going into Allegiant and he was so absent through the entire story that I just didn't care at all when he went into a coma, and I again didn't care at all when they pulled the plug on him.<br /><br />***<br /><br />Allegiant was about what I expected.<br /><br />After Insurgent, I think Allegiant was exactly what I expected it to be. I don't feel like Tris and Tobias's relationship ever \\"healed\\", and for the rest I was just mildly indifferent. Throw in a few other problems, and it's about what I expected it to be.<br /><br />I still look back on Divergent fondly, but I think overall I just don't like where the series went. I hated how it destroyed Tris and Tobias's relationship, I didn't love where the plot went, and I hated the ending Roth chose.

I choose him over and over again, and he chooses me.\\"<br /><br />i loved this series. the sparse writing, world building...all of it. i'll miss it.<br /><br />and for now, i'm still kind of grieving.

B009NFHF0Q|5.0|The Ocean at the End of the Lane is a novel by Neil Gaiman with fantasy elements. I could say that it's a fantasy novel, but that doesn't seem perfectly right. If anything, it's a story of childhood, and many things seemed magical back then.<br /><br />The story is told from the perspective of our unnamed protagonist who has returned to his hometown for a funeral. While there, he finds himself wandering about until he ends up at the old Hempstock farm where he remembers having a friend named Lettie. He takes a chance and asks the resident if she's still around - although it's not immediately clear if he's talking to Lettie's mother or Lettie's grandmother. Given Lettie isn't around, he goes on to see the duck pond at the back of the house that she used to refer to as her ocean. And then the memories begin.<br /><br />He remembers a time when he was much younger and Lettie was about eleven. They first met because a lodger had stolen his father's car and had driven it down the lane. The car had come to rest near the Hempstock farm and the car thief was dead inside the vehicle. This event was followed by strange occurrences of people coming into money or money being found in strange places like inside a fish. And when the protagonist wakes up with a coin lodged in his throat, he then turns to Lettie for help. And for some reason, the Hempstock women are indeed in a position to help.<br /><br />The reason I'm somewhat hesitant to label this as an outright fantasy novel is the interesting and gradual pace that we are introduced to the more supernatural elements of this story. Instead of being thrust right into a world with magic, we instead are immersed in the history of our narrator - thus we are immersed in his childhood. And this setup is done quite well in such a fashion that helps the reader find himself right there holding Lettie's Hempstocks hand as we walk through her farmstead.<br /><br />And the ultimate &#34;villain&#34; in this story isn't even purely evil, but yet another creature acting on its nature. And that's one of the things I always appreciate about these stories as well. It's not passing moral judgement on the characters whether &#34;good&#34; or &#34;evil&#34; - instead everyone is acting on their roles in the greater scheme of things. And thus that leaves you as a reader wondering whether or not you can really look down on certain characters because they just can't help who they are.<br /><br />The tone of this book is definitely a lot like other Gaiman stories like Coraline or MirrorMask. But at the same time this is not a &#34;children's book&#34; just because the story is centered around a child - something that can also be said of many Neil Gaiman stories. I can imagine some folks in the &#34;young adult&#34; market reading this book and enjoying it while any adult will find something in this book as well. After all, we were all once children before. We all feared the dark and what mysterious things we imagined lived under our beds. And this book manages to tap into that part of our shared history.<br /><br />The Ocean at the End of the Lane is a magical book that transports you back to a simpler time, back to your childhood and back to your more primitive or baser fears. It's elegant in its simplicity and on the whole just a wonderful read.ut yet another creature acting on its nature. And that's one of the things I always appreciate about these stories as well. It's not passing moral judgement on the characters whether &#34;good&#34; or &#34;evil&#34; - instead everyone is acting on their roles in the greater scheme of things. 006202406X|2.0|This review contains spoilers.  Read at your own risk.  I will start by saying I have read Allegiant twice.  (I know, I'm nuts).  I hated it the first time I read it so I gave it a few days to sink in and thought, maybe I need to read it again to see if I still hate it... here's what I've finally decided.<br /><br />1.  As many have already pointed out the dual POV had so much potential, but it really didn't work.  I wish Roth had stuck with Tris' POV.  It's what readers were expecting given the first two books and if she had ALWAYS planned on the end she did, then it should NEVER have been narrated from Tris's POV.<br /><br />2.  The ending.  I actually appreciate and understand the \\"bigger picture\\" that Roth was trying to convey.  The problem was that it was poorly done.  There was little build up, no real convincing the reader that it HAD to be Tris and ultimately she really didn't HAVE to kill Tris to convey the ultimate selflessness.  It could have been done so many other ways.  In fact, just that Tris volunteered for an unworthy person was enough.  She didn't have to die to accomplish the idea of self sacrifice for the right reasons.  So I applaud Roth's ultimate motive, but think her execution was greatly flawed.<br /><br />3.  Plot holes.  For the ending to work it had to be THE only way right?  If I recall correctly, didn't Amity HAVE memory serum for those from Chicago who \\"went past the line\\"?  So why not just steal some from Amity and release it in the compound.  That plot hole along with MANY others makes this ending hard to swallow for the reader.  In addition, Tris wasn't even killed by a great villain.  We hardly know David, let alone really hate him...Now, if Roth hadn't killed off Jeanine in Insurgent and there was still some great power struggle between Tris and Jeanine in Allegiant that would have made for a much better plot, imo.  And I won't even get started on how many issues I have with the plot regarding Natalie (Tris' mother), Marcus, and Evelyn...<br /><br />4.  Slow pacing.  It was a bit boring to read.  I know there was a lot of information to give us about the outside world, but really, it dragged on.  The two trips to the fringe added nothing to my understanding and made it a bit more muddled if anything.  Where was all the action like we saw from the first two books?  And where are our beloved (and hated) characters from the first two?  And why not redeem Peter and Caleb?  That would have been interesting to read about and given hope that people CAN change for the better.  And personally I find it hard that Abnegation raised Caleb would be so utter selfish at the end... I kind of think SOME of his Abnegation would have come through, even if he was a big coward overall.<br /><br />So in summary I didn't hate the book.  But I didn't love it.  And I won't be rereading it a third time... I'm not that crazy.  I'm not swearing off reading anything by Roth again, but I will be very hesitant to invest so much into anything by her.  I believe she has the potential to write great stories (like Divergent), and I sincerely hope this is a hard lesson learned about the thoroughness of her plot and writing style but one that she will learn from regardless.

There were often times I had to look back at the beginning of the chapter to see who's POV it was being told from. The personalities of Tris and Tobias were totally watered down to the point I couldn't tell which one was narrating a given chapter. Every other chapter is alternated between being told by Tris and Tobias. A good writer does not need to tell you who's POV the story is being told from, at times I felt like I was reading a bad fanfiction.<br /><br />The plot was substandard. I liked how the other two books addressed the dissidence between the factions, and how Tris was breaking that mold, by not taking on the personality of one faction vs. another. The people in the city were merely \\"puppets\\" whose strings were being pulled by those at the bureau. Such should be encouraged, or perhaps forced, two watch DiCaprio's two most powerful early performances: as Johnny Depp's retarded brother in What's Eating Gilbert Grape? and as author Jim Carroll in The Basketball Diaries. DiCaprio's career may have taken a huge turn for the stupid, but in these two films, he proves himself a consummate professional.<br /><br />I never associated the Jim Carroll of The Basketball Diaries, somehow, with the Jim Carroll whose 1985 cult hit &#34;People Who Died&#34; has become a mainstay of the classic-rock frat-house drinking party crowd. And the plot of TBD pretty much parallells the first couple of verses of the song; if you've got the hang of one, you'll get the hang of the other.<br /><br />Carroll was a basketball player at a New York catholic school in the late seventies, being scouted by the majors along with a couple of his friends, growing up poor but honest, and an aspiring writer to boot. Not the world's most notable existence, but a good one, until the flirting with drugs that he and his three friends did got out of hand. The turning point is the death of his friend Bobby (Michael Imperioli, last seen trying and failing to save Summer of Sam) from leukemia at 16. From there, Jim, Pedro (James Madio), and Mickey (a young Mark Wahlberg, in the role that took him from cute boy-band idol into serious actor-ville) descend ever deeper into the world of heroin addiction, while their erstwhile friend Neutron (Patrick McGraw) serves as a yardstick of what might have been had they stayed clean and sober.<br /><br />DiCaprio shines. Wahlberg shines. See this ASAP. Flirting with a position on the top 100 list. **** ½

006202406X|5.0|This book was amazing, even with the sad ending, and let's face it, this story had no happy ending coming for it. yes, of course we all wanted the protagonists to live happily ever after, but the writer was mature and told the story instead of giving the fans everything they wanted. That, my friends, is the mark of an excellent writer, not giving every story a happy ending so they get great sales by love-crazed pre-teen readers. Sometimes, a story matures into an unhappy ending, and even if the world is saved for everyone else, not everyone in the story can have a perfect ending, least of all the ones who fought the war against the evils of the story. Of course it was horrible, of course it was sad, of course it was heart-breaking for those of us who love and trust these characters like real people, but it was the story that needed to be told. The author is a vehicle for a story, and this story didn't have a happy ending. Get over it. Lots of brilliant books don't have happy endings, or lose important characters or separate lovers in the last moments. His Dark Materials (Golden Compass series), The Inheritance Cycle (Eragon series), just to name a couple popular book series that did NOT have happy ending and yet are considered excellent books by their fans. It may take us a long time to be able to re-read this book without feeling sad, it took me a long time to re-read the 6th Harry Potter book after Dumbledore's death (I, at the time, vowed to never read it again) but in learning to cope with the death of a beloved character, I learned a lesson of how to deal with losses of ideals and dreams in real life too. That is the purpose of this book, to show that strong people are remembered by their deeds, even in an imperfect world where everything we do may not matter on a grand scale, it still matters to those who loved us and those we leave behind. I will always love this series for the strength it gave me during a difficult time in my life through Tris's choice to join the Dauntless, to forsake her family and everything she had ever known to be her own person after trying for so long to be someone who she never could be. I loved this book for it's quiet, waiting, lost and tired pace, it's airport setting (a literal waiting landscape, so perfect a setting for the tone of book). I loved this book for it's small romantic bits, not light enough to be insincere or clumsy, yet not heavy enough to be smut or trashy. I loved this book for telling us that we may be different and we may be technologically advanced, but that no one can tell us that we are better or worse for what is inside of us, our genetics or ancestry, only for our deeds.  I loved this book for it's stark and dark contrast to the previous 2, but still following the story and sending us all on a ride to it's shocking, but necessary ending. Yes, I loved the ending. Yes, I cried reading it, but I knew immediately it was for the best and that it was the story that needed to be told. I loved the subtle nature of it, not long and drawn out or gory, but simple, clean, and fast, just as the character always was, in life and death. I was sad, I was devastated, I was heartbroken, but I knew the ending was perfect as I read it, and I wouldn't change a thing, not even to satisfy my own heart. So don't listen to the pre-teen haters who will tell you not to appreciate the gravity of this story just because it has a sad ending. Don't listen to those who will tell you the ending is wrong, when they aren't the author, were not the vehicle for telling the story like she was. Don't listen to those who will tell you not to read this book because the pace or the tone is not what they wanted. I say read, read and appreciate, and learn, and let your own mind and heart decide if it was worth your while to read. Most of all, don't be afraid to love a story just because it ends or didn't end the way you wanted it to, any less than you love a person because they lived a different life than you wanted or died before you thought it was their time. I say love the story for everything it is and isn't.

B00CA3C1N4|5.0|The concept has seen numerous iterations, but the basic mechanics are always the same: worlds pump out ships at a steady rate, you send those ships at other worlds, and conquer the galaxy.  The important features are in the details:<br />worlds are linked to each other (by the titular &#34;star links&#34;) and ships can only be sent along those links.  No random assaults coming from the opposite side of the galaxy here - there are distinct front lines of battle.<br />Some worlds have special features, like increased production rate or decreased rate of sending out ships, which helps to create desirable targets and limited avenues of attack.<br />The most important feature about Starlink is activated abilities, such as the ability to destroy a starlink, to temporarily halt an enemy fleet's ability to capture your world, or to give one of your fleets vastly improved combat effectiveness.  You only have a limited number of ability points to choose the abilities you want to have available during a game, so there's a lot of combinations to try out, which helps keep gameplay varied.  My only complaint is that it makes you grind for ability points - you get xp after a game which levels you up.  I don't really care for that mechanic, but others may disagree.<br /><br />Oh, and the map generator isn't always balanced.  It's entirely possible for one player to spawn on or right next to one of those worlds that has double production rate, which is a major advantage at the beginning of a game.  But eh, It's Galcon.  Not like it's Civilization or something.  The occasional imbalanced start isn't that big a deal.

B00BD99JMW|1.0|I finished this last night and afterwards I felt just dead inside. I absolutely hate when a series ends leaving me feeling unsatisfied and even, in this case, angry. I feel kind of betrayed by the author.<br /><br />I loved Divergent. The book had its faults, but I really fell in love with the whole story, the characters, the romance between Tris and Four, the Factions, etc. I read and re-read Divergent probably 8-10 times. I bought the audio book version as well. I was SO PSYCHED about the movie! I bought Insurgent and devoured it as well. It didn't have quite the same magic for me that Divergent did, but I still loved it. So I was REALLY psyched to read Allegiant.<br /><br />And maybe my hopes were too high.<br /><br />I don't know.<br /><br />That doesn't change the fact that this book utterly and completely disappointed me.<br /><br />SPOILERS AHEAD<br /><br />For real, SPOILERS!<br /><br />READ AT YOUR OWN RISK!<br /><br />[<br /><br />**What's outside the fence?**<br /><br />I was really excited, after reading the 'cliffhanger' ending to Insurgent, to find out what was outside the fence. I had my theories, and I was right about some, wrong about others. I was expecting the cameras and Truman Show-esque thing, but I was not expecting all the genetic manipulation crap. As soon as our heroes get outside the fence, there are chapters and chapters of info dumping and, to be honest, it's kind of dull. We learn that Chicago (and some other cities) were created as 'experiments' because of genetic manipulation gone wrong. Supposedly these cities were an attempt at creating more 'genetically pure' people (aka Divergents). This is so far-fetched and bizarre, but I was willing to go along with it.<br /><br />**Tris and Four**<br /><br />I wasn't against the dual perspective, though once I finished the book I realized why she HAD to write it in a dual perspective. However, I don't think it was done well. There were several times where I would get halfway through a chapter and not remember who I was in the head of at the time, mostly because the two narrators had identical voices.<br /><br />Tris has always been an interesting character to me. I really liked that she was tough, but a little vulnerable and naive. I liked her progression through Divergent and Insurgent. She doesn't really progress much here. We've always known Tris is a selfless person who is more than willing to sacrifice herself for the greater good, or for her loved ones. That doesn't change here. More on that later. She does become kind of arrogant and holier-than-thou here, and it really started to bug me. She's 16, but she often acts (and everyone treats) her like a grown-up. She is pretty much always right in Allegiant and she makes sure everyone knows it.<br /><br />Four was awesome in Divergent. He loses all of that awesomeness in Allegiant. He becomes quite whiny and pathetic to the point where I actually wanted Tris to break up with him. She probably should have, considering some of the stupid and completely out of character stuff he does. I really hated seeing him reduced to this quivering mess of a boy who does nothing but wax poetic about his mommy and daddy issues. Four was always strong, and he is the polar opposite of that here.<br /><br />I did enjoy the progression of their relationship though. Some people have complained about the scene where Four accuses Tris of being jealous, but I actually liked it. I like that they, FOR ONCE, acted like TEENAGERS. Unfortunately that was a tiny part of this book, and for the rest of it they act like 30-40 year old worldly adults.<br /><br />**THE END OF THE CONFLICT**<br /><br />So we have Four's mother Evelyn running Chicago like a factionless tyrannical dictatorship. Johanna and Marcus running the Allegiant, trying to reinstate the factions.<br /><br />Evelyn has been portrayed as a nearly heartless person, hell bent on making all the Factioned people clean toilets like the Factionless had to do for so many years. At the height of the conflict, she is willing to allow a &#34;Death Serum&#34; to kill pretty much everyone in Chicago in order to avoid going back to the Factions. This is when Four arrives and says &#34;Hey Mom, I'll be your son again if you stop acting like a monster&#34; and she goes &#34;Okay.&#34;<br /><br />She then negotiates with Marcus and Johanna. Marcus wants to take over as evil dictator and Johanna says &#34;Nah, you're not gonna do that&#34; and Marcus just says &#34;Okay.&#34;<br /><br />Conflict over.<br /><br />WHAT?<br /><br />WHAT????????????<br /><br />**Mass Murder**<br /><br />Roth has not shied away from killing off characters in the first two books, but I felt like most of that had a purpose. Tris' parents in the first book, Al (due to the guilt over his own actions towards Tris), Jeannine in Insurgent, and more. Deaths in Allegiant come just as rapidly and have even less meaning. Tori dies in a very sudden manner, and then is mostly forgotten about. She was referred to as the leader of the Dauntless, but she is killed and then is nothing more than an afterthought for the rest of the book.<br /><br />Uriah's death is given a lot more page time, but only as a way to make Four feel like total garbage for getting involved in the rebellion and to make Tris &#34;right&#34; once again.<br /><br />The biggest death of all was Tris, and this was the biggest disappointment to me. I will be straight up honest - I LIKE a happy ending. I read YA because I like knowing that things will most likely end up happy overall. I read romance because I know there will always be a happily ever after. HOWEVER, I can deal with a bittersweet ending so long as it feels satisfying and feels like closure.<br /><br />Tris' death was NOT that ending.<br /><br />Caleb's betrayal was a huge part of Insurgent, and that continues on in Allegiant. He has a LOT to atone for in Allegiant and when he volunteers for the suicide mission to help save everyone, he does it because he wants Tris to forgive him. And he doesn't want to live with the guilt of what he did. Instead of letting him, Tris forces him to let her go instead. I understand this. Tris forgave him and doesn't want him to die because he feels guilty. I get it.<br /><br />HOWEVER<br /><br />Caleb doesn't get that opportunity to redeem himself. While I understand that Tris acted the way she had to act, that doesn't mean Caleb can't take a bullet for her. That doesn't mean he can't force a redemption. Instead, Tris dies in a completely unsatisfying scene that left me going &#34;WHAT THE HECK JUST HAPPENED?&#34; Caleb lives and basically still is the coward and traitor.<br /><br />Caleb NEEDED to redeem himself. He needed to take that bullet for Tris and die.<br /><br />Instead, we get this messed up ending where Caleb lives. Peter lives (gets his mind erased BY HIS OWN CHOICE and gets to start over). Marcus lives (just disappears somehow). David (Tris' murderer) lives (also gets his mind erased and even though he's an evil murderer, no one cares because he doesn't remember). Almost all the bad guys live and get to have perfectly happy, normal lives.<br /><br />This is NOT SATISFYING.<br /><br />THIS IS POINTLESS.<br /><br />Sorry, but this book was awful. I wanted to love it. I REALLY wanted to love it. I was willing to deal with all the weird genetically damaged stuff. I was willing to deal with the complete dismantling of Four's character. I was willing to deal with a lot, but Tris' pointless and needless death... NOPE. This death felt like it was here for shock value only. So the author could be &#34;edgy&#34; and &#34;different.&#34;<br /><br />Unfortunately, all she did for me is ruin the entire series. I won't be re-reading Divergent or Insurgent anymore. I won't be re-reading Allegiant. And I definitely won't be seeing the movies that I was once so excited for. Knowing how depressingly it ends ruins it all for mes. At the height of the conflict, she is willing to allow a &#34;Death Serum&#34; to kill pretty much everyone in Chicago in order to avoid going back to the Factions. This is when Four arrives and says &#34;Hey Mom, I'll be your son again if you stop acting like a monster&#34; and she goes &#34;Okay.&#34;<br /><br />She then negotiates with Marcus and Johanna. 006202406X|1.0|I would firstly say that I am a big fan of the series. I picked up this book when it first arrived. I waited for the 2nd and enjoyed it and then I waited the conclusion of the series. Upon reading the book cover to cover I felt victimized. It read more like teenage fan fiction rather than an established novelist. I feel that the first two books were very strong and mature but this one was not. It read again like a teenagers fan fiction. I think that Mrs. Roth could have created a very wonderful wrap up to the series but instead opted for a rushed, messy and chaotic end to the series. I tried to understand this ending and how it flowed with the overall themes of the story but in the end it does not make sense.<br /><br />There were some very wonderful surprises in the book. There were very well placed comedic aspects and evil themes and characters. It certainly went a little dark but right when it was hitting its stride it would revert to fan fiction. You can tell that there are some things that felt very forced. There were some character choices and backstory that seemed more forced and were just there to appease a small group of fans.<br /><br />She tried to make it sort of like George R.R. But I think Tris' relationship with Four, despite the fact that she thinks she's in love with him, just seemed too contrived and maybe Roth couldn't write it any other way. The whole plot, the relationships that had been built over the first two books, just collapsed in this one.<br /><br />And frankly, with that ending, Roth pretty much guaranteed that I will never read this series again.

B004ZNAXX2|5.0|Pink Floyd were the soundtrack to my adolescence. I first purchased The Wall on glorious vinyl. Naturally I got the CD when it first came out, and now this amazing box set. As one of the most demanding bands around in terms of sound quality and presentation values, I always respected Pink Floyd (and in particular Roger Waters) for producing consistently high-quality releases. So here we have the Immersion box set of The Wall. Before getting to the discs themselves, I have to note that the packaging is, as always with Floyd, magnificent. Wall-themed items include a scarf, marbles, and coasters, along with a replica concert ticket and backstage pass. An over-sized booklet of drafts of album art as well as photos from the concerts really round out the experience.<br /><br />Onto the music...the show must go on! After getting this box set, I listened to the original album's remaster from \\"...we came in?\\" to \\"Isn't this where...\\" The remastered version of the original album is tremendous. David Gilmour's guitar tracks are noticeably improved; in fact, hearing the opening riff of \\"In the Flesh\\" is stunningly fresh. On \\"The Thin Ice\\", Nick Mason's drums are much clearer. On other tracks, Roger Waters' thumping bass is solidly enhanced from earlier releases. The vocals of both Waters and Gilmour are crystal clear; Gilmour's soothing singing at the start of \\"The Thin Ice\\" and Waters' mournful tone during \\"Vera Lynn\\" are absolutely crisp. And on it goes, providing the same seamless story in a new and improved acoustic package.<br /><br />The demo discs are a treasure trove. On first listen or to the casual observer, the demo tracks might seem short and incoherent. But taken as a whole, they provide amazing insight into how the individual band members experimented with different tones and sounds to create such a seamless piece as The Wall. Gilmour experiments quite a bit over the course of the demos, and Waters plays around with the lyrics to get it \\"just right.\\" I found it quite interesting and enjoyable to hear the songs evolve from demos to rough mixes to the final album versions. Listening to the demos straight through and then listening to the remastered album from start to finish is as the title states and immersion.<br /><br />The live album \\"Is There Anybody Out There\\" was previously released, but is still a welcome part of the Immersion set. The documentary \\"Behind The Wall\\" is an interesting watch, giving a much better sense of the extensive work that went on behind the production of The Wall concerts in 1980 and 1981. It's also interesting to compare the travails of those shows to Waters' production of The Wall live in the last couple years. Building the wall in 1980/81 was a tedious and at times dangerous endeavor for the roadies. How far the technology has come. I also enjoyed the footage of the original live opening where other people made to look (somewhat) like the members of Pink Floyd took the stage. Very cool to watch and a brilliant piece of performance art.<br /><br />All in all, the Immersion box set of The Wall is exactly what you would expect: a very well-produced package that does indeed immerse you into the experience of Pink Floyd's The Wall.ite interesting and enjoyable to hear the songs evolve from demos to rough mixes to the final album versions. Listening to the demos straight through and then listening to the remastered album from start to finish is as the title states and immersion.<br /><br />The live album \\"Is There Anybody Out There\\" was previously released, but is still a welcome part of the Immersion set. The documentary \\"Behind The Wall\\" is an interesting watch, giving a much better sense of the extensive work that went on behind the production of The Wall concerts in 1980 and 1981. It's also interesting to compare the travails of those shows to Waters' production of The Wall live in the last couple years. Building the wall in 1980/81 was a tedious and at times dangerous endeavor for the roadies. How far the technology has come. I also enjoyed the footage of the original live opening where other people made to look (somewhat) like the members of Pink Floyd took the stage. Very cool to watch and a brilliant piece of performance art.<br /><br />All in all, the Immersion box set of The Wall is exactly what you would expect: a very well-produced package that does indeed immerse you into the experience of Pink Floyd's The Wall.

First and foremost, she needs to understand that this is the entertainment industry. People want to be entertained, not depressed. It is all about being able to write exactly what you want to convey you message and then sell that product to prospective readers.  Second, she needs to develop a better grasp of her target market and write to that target's expectations, thus building a loyal following of readers. And these are people which specifically came to see her.  Lastly, IT IS her prerogative to tell the story the way she wants to.  Although this is true, I will caution that there are ramifications to exercising that freedom, and deviating from the expectations of the target market.  This is exactly the reason why the Dixie Chicks fell from the top of the country music charts.  Their music was top notch, but what they chose to say and do (their entitled rights) outside of their music directly alienated Nashville and most country music fans. Accordingly, as is the audience's prerogative, everyone turned them off.<br /><br />I believe she owes us an explanation (and maybe even a re-write). There is so much more she could have done. Oh it has the standard &#34;diverse cast&#34; that everyone loves, and it's got some not-quite-lame special effects.  But the dialog is iffy, at best.  At the beginning, some commander dude says something like, &#34;We've just done the impossible.  And that makes us special.&#34;  Now, even Sheldon would have trouble with this logic, because if you've done something, it can't be impossible, unless of course you're in an alternate universe where the rules of Physics are different.  &#34;Well, they didn't do anything impossible then, and therefore they may not be special,&#34; would be my response.<br /><br />At another point, something bad happens and someone else says,&#34;Good God in Heaven.&#34;  I agree with that.  &#34;Good God in Heaven, why did they ever create this series?&#34; would be my question, not &#34;Good God why did Mr. Murdoch ever cancel the series?&#34;<br /><br />My apologies to Sheldon.  Kinda.  And leave Rupert Murdoch -- and Leonard -- alone.

One of the practical considerations, of course, is that by the sheer weight of the numbers, if Darwinian evolution occurs spontaneously whenever and wherever conditions are right, there should be literally millions of worlds in the observable universe where it has (and/or is) happening, with many civilizations far more advanced than ours (per the somewhat controversial work of astronomer Frank Drake and others). B00BD99JMW|3.0|**Spoilers**<br /><br />Allegiant is proving to be quite the controversy, though perhaps not for the reasons it should. The series was never going to be fine literature, though I was definitely entertained and devoured the first two books; they were escapist and overall a good read. After reading Insurgent I found out that Veronica Roth is quite vocal about her Christian background, which set up certain expectations for the climax of the final book (in more ways that one, you might say). However, I didn't find her tone at all preachy or heavy-handed in the first two novels - that changes a bit here. This final book has definite changed how I'm going to view the movies as they come out, and will make it hard to read the first two books again.<br /><br />I had absolutely no ideas about what might happen in Allegiant, so I was excited to find out how the series would conclude. However, I think there were missteps - some apparent right from the beginning. From a narrative standpoint, my biggest qualm was the shift in perspective; while I didn't mind seeing things through Four's eyes, he is not the person who led me into this world, and is not the most reliable or interesting narrator. There was also hardly any narrative between Tris and Four - often I read a few pages into a chapter before realizing I was picturing the wrong person. Switching back and forth every other chapter is a bit hacky - check out how George RR Martin does it to get an idea of how multiple perspective is used skillfully. I see now why Roth decided to do this, but I think she could have cut of Tris's voice and ended with Four's and we still would have had the same journey.<br /><br />The actual storyline also frustrated me a bit; I was rather disappointed to find out what was outside the city because it took some of the magic away. Everything slows around the middle of the book, and I found myself wondering how it could possibly be wrapped up in a cohesive way (which, in truth, it doesn't quite do). Most of the book is far away from the world that was created in the first two books, which is a risky choice. I definitely saw more of Roth's religious background in this book as well (quite obviously in Tris' questioning of God and the destruction of her reality).<br /><br />Which leads me to the end - the focal point for 99% of the reviews up so far. From a purely literary standpoint, I don't have a problem with the main character dying. We readers are so used to happy endings that we forget how often a narrative can't logically conclude with the heroine dancing into the sunset, but I really don't think this is why Tris died. I thin Roth made a calculated choice that was consistent with her views, but not with the text, or rather, what we all bought into in the text. Tris' death was very symbolic (the Christ imagery is a little overdone here) but unsatisfying. I kept expecting her to pop out in the last few chapters - a sign that the death was not fully resolved. Make no mistake, the heart of the series went with Tris, which is why so many people are finding it hard to take, I think.<br /><br />You definitely need to suspend reality to believe the ending has any sort of permanence, that Tris died to save something real - lose that and the ending stings that much more. I don't think the book deserves a 1 star rating, but I, for one, can't get fully behind a series that ends on a bum note. In Game of Thrones, it's tragic when a character meets their grisly end, but there are other full-formed characters who can carry the book. The biggest problem here is that Four is not fully-developed; we see him from Tris' eyes, and when we lose that it all feels a little hollow.  I also just didn't expect the book to take the turn it did when it pulled the rug out from the first two books, and I'm not sure I liked it - we've seen the genetics allegory before, and done better.<br /><br />To sum up: There isn't enough narrative weight in Allegiant to support the bold moves thatRoth makes. The storyline is a bit lacking compared to the first two novels, and at the end of the day, the ideas expressed here feel out of place - these readers (myself included) just wanted to happy ending! But hey, it didn't offend me and it got me thinking, so maybe that alone makes it a little successful.

B00DJ80VLS|3.0|I'm so sorry to have to report that this may be my least favorite Dream Theater album ever.  I have every single one including When Dream and Day Unite, and that one had lots of forgettable music on it, sorry guys, but this new self-titled album has so much wrong with it I wonder what the band was truly aiming for.<br /><br />First off, the recording quality is dismal.  We were told repeatedly that Mangini did such crazy, amazing drumming on this album and even the band was blown away by how insane it was.  Terrific!  I can't wait!  Oh, I'm listening to 80% guitars/keyboards and 20% bass and drums.  They smashed this &#34;crazy/insane/amazing&#34; drumming so far down in the mix and muddied it so badly I can barely hear what the hell is going on.  Mike Mangini himself said that he used &#34;a bunch of triggers&#34; i.e., foot pedals and small trigger pads all around his already sizable drum kit to pull off these crazy, impossible fills that can't be done without them.  He goes on about how he used foot triggers to play cymbals while he did some ridiculous fill that otherwise wouldn't be able to played without them!  Wow, Mike, did you hear the final mix?  I can't hear ANY of this stuff you say you played.  Your drums sound like the engineer hated you and stuck a single mic in the NEXT ROOM and told you to go ahead and play.  The snare sounds like bucket you got at Home depot.  The bass drums sound a wet blanket hitting the wall.  All of the toms sound like you stole them from Phil Collins from the 80's.  The truth of the matter is your amazing technical prowess has been squashed under a layer of other instruments so completely you might as well have sat recording out and let Cakewalk Pro Audio do your job.  Now, the entire album is not like this, just about 99% of it.  I don't know who convinced you that from the Dramatic Turn of Events album to this self-titled album that you should make your snare go from that very cool crisp snap that you had to this awful thud,but you've been had, and again, your inspiring perfection has been lost in a sea of overtones and muddy sound.<br /><br />There was this really cool part near the end minute or so of Enigma Machine where you played a pretty neat solo fill and I could hear some absolutely astounding things, and then the rest of the band came in and smooshed it all in a small box and you were back to being stepped on by guitars and keyboards.<br /><br />Lemme harp on Mr. Myung for a minute, too.  You are a fantastic player.  You approach the intrument much in the same way I've approached instruments.  Practice, practice, practice is what makes us truly good players.  We work very hard at what we do.  You are at the very top of your game and I still don't understand why you are mixed so far down in the music as to be nearly non-existant.  You're almost like a prog-bass version of Michael Anthony at this point, except the keys AND guitars are drowning you out.  Your playing is amazing and I CAN'T HEAR YOU!  Maybe it's the engineers, maybe it's the Bongo Bass?  I dunno.  I'm not saying go get a Rickenbaker or anything but maybe you could try one and see what kinda difference it makes?  Or, better yet, get that mothballed Chapman Stick back out and beat that sucker up like on New Millenium.  I could hear you back then and it was awesome!<br /><br />I'm not going to bother reviewing individual songs, but I'll say that the high point of the album is clearly Illumination Theory.  This single (albeit long) song has all the energy and excitement I've come to love about Dream Theater.  It's sadly, however, the only song, after sitting through the entire rest of the album, to give me those &#34;electric chills&#34; you sometimes get when hearing certain musical refrains.  I listened intently for that spark through every song and very early in Illumination Theory I got supercharged because it was just that good.  Unfortunately, the rest of the album left me feeling like I was hearing a cheap copyof Dream Theater and most of what they were playing was almost a parody of itself.<br /><br />I guess it's going to be at least another year and a half or two years before the next Dream Theater album comes out and I'm very sorry to say that gone are the days of high energy and outright exultation from the amazing album that was Systematic Chaos.  Dream Theater was one of the few bands were every new album (unintentionally) sought to leave the previous album in the dust and Systematic Chaos, to me, seemed to be the pinacle of this near constant upward trend.  Other bands after three or four albums hit their high and never seemed to get back to that same level due to personnel changes or mainstream influence; Yes and Rush are two pretty good examples.  They still write music and release albums but there's something about those older albums that keeps us going back.  Dream Theater bucks that trend and I enjoy Train of Thought or Octavarium just as much as Images and Words, but the trend tripped badly with Black Clouds and Silver Linings.  Count of Tuscany was a wonderful song but the rest falls by the wayside.  There's about 45 seconds of truly awesome music in Dramatic Turn in the second verse of Lost Not Forgotten but the rest is just a pointless storm of notes.<br /><br />If you're a huge fan, you'll get this anyway.  If you need to determine if you're a fan and are trying to figure out if the self-titled album is right for you, I almost hate to have to say to pass on this one.  Stick with anything starting at Images and Words and stop when you hit Systematic Chaos and never go forward from there.  I think Dream Theater is on a slippery slope they may not recover from and it truly hurts my heart to have to say it.

006202406X|5.0|**Spoilers**I finished Alligent around 10 last night and basically stared in silence for a couple of hours.  I've battled the gamut of grief throughout the night and woke up with new perspective.<br /><br />All in all I think I'm landing on \\"hauntingly beautiful\\" as my best description.  Sure, everyone loves a \\"happy ending\\" and yes...many (myself included) read these types of books for escape and balk at the authors who do not give us the alternate realities we desire.  But rarely, when we reflect deeply enough we are able to also find threads of redemption and cathartic meaning in works of fiction that enage and surprise us.  This final installment did just that.<br /><br />While I could reflect on the circular revelations of each key character, my thoughts primarily land on Tris and Tobias.<br /><br />From the first chapter of Divergent on we see Tris as someone who lives with this deep pitted hole for the lack of selflessness she feels.  While she has countless opportunities throughout the series to display sacrifice, it's never really evident to her (though obvious to those around her) that her actions are truly unselfish and brave.  It's not until her final act when she truly wants to live, has something to live for, yet gives her life in exchange for others, that she is able to yield to her personal measure of selflessness and release herself.  My hope that is in death she finally made peace with this...and I think she does with her final statement, \\"I believe it.\\"<br /><br />As for Tobias, his final words and thoughts were gut wrenching passages to digest...(esp. the scene with Christina re: the memory serum).  When his character is introduced and progresses, we see a picture of someone desperately broken.  He was damaged not by his genes, but by the wounds inflicted by those closest to him that chose to hurt and abandon him.  Ultimately and (ironically) he only finds healing (mending) through hurt and loss.  Note that Tris' parting message to him was not \\"Ilove you,\\" but \\"I didn't WANT to leave you\\".  There is power in that...yes, loss, but also redemptive power.<br /><br />Last year, I lost a friend to a tragic accident.  Over the course of time I have watched her sweet husband grieve her absence.  His honest pain, anger, and yes...his ability to remember her and the impact she had on his life and the lives of others has created healing.  The pain is still there, but there is untold strength in the mending.  Nothing any of us would have chosen, but an immeasurable picture of true, gritty..bravery.  There is so much power in Tobias' final words in the last chapter re: step by step, choosing to put one foot in front of the other and live life kind of bravery.  Some times we get to choose our path and sometimes it chooses us.  What we do with it is where we discover our truest identity and meaning.<br /><br />I for one am thanking Veronica Roth for this bittersweet, redemptive end to a series and characters who captured my heart.

B00BD99JMW|5.0|SPOILERS BELOW!!*********************<br />4 or 5 stars? Well, I am heartbroken. For sure. But, I am not sure I am completely disappointed. Did I get what I hoping for out of this book? No. I must have read Divergent and Insurgent at least 7 times. I read them quickly, with excitement and adrenaline, and I read them slowly trying to relish every word. As I read Allegiant, I sometimes rushed along with the momentum, thinking that I will re-read and take the time to truly think about each sentence next time. But, I honestly do not think I can re-live that again. I forced myself to finish the book, not wanting to believe what had happened. I wish this book was released after the Divergent film, because I am not even sure I can put myself through this tale again knowing of Tris' death.<br />Do I think it was the wrong ending? I'm not so sure. I like happily ever afters. A lot of reviewers have applauded this ending for being messy and brave, and difficult. But, we have plenty of dramas and heartache in our lives. When I read a book, or watch a movie, I do steer towards feel-good ones, even if they are not realistic. It is a nice escape, and I don't think there is anything wrong with wanting that.  I also think, that in this case, it would have been interesting to see Tris face a normal life. As long as we have known her, she has had a fire within her to do something big and meaningful. And she was amazing! But, her whole existence was to be an instigator. To intervene, make bold choices, to take risks, make sacrifices, and try to fix things. She was told over and over again that she was unique, special, and powerful. I think it would have been more challenging for her, more brave even, to just be normal in the end. I say this because I think that is a part of the process for most young adults becoming adults. So many of us are told that we can be whatever we want to be, and do whatever we want to do, and that is great. Every child should have the self-confidence to follow their dreams. But, the reality is that we do not all grow up to be the astronaut, the NFL player, the movie star, or the Nobel Prize winner that we dreamed we would be. Instead of feeling like failures, or being disappointed with ourselves, at some point the majority of us have to be brave enough to be ok with just being normal. We have to find contentment and happiness with what we do achieve, and choose to make a difference in even the smallest ways, because it is cumulative. I think that would have been Tris' grown up moment. She was not invincible, and she could have chosen to be content with the work she had done, and worked toward change in a less self destructive way. It would have been a significant character development to show, and she never got there. So, I regret that for her. But, she was a hero. Maybe that was the best she could offer the others. And it certainly was the most harrowing and dramatic contribution to the story.<br />There is a part of me that does find closure with this story though, and respect for Veronica Roth's story telling. Tris' motivations were strongly linked to her mother all along. While she also thought about what her father would want her to do, with every new revelation she made about her mother in Divergent and Insurgent, you could see her direct her actions toward emulating her. Throughout Insurgent, she was focused on finishing her mother's work. In Allegiant, as she uncovered the rest of her mother's story, it was clear what she truly felt she had to do. She had no other choice. It was always her driving force. I thought her mother's choice to be \\"normal\\" and live in Abnegation instead of finishing her work to help the genetically damaged in the Fringe was extremely compelling. I don't know if Natalie was taking the harder path to turn away from the conflict outside and try to be \\"normal\\", or if it was easier for her to choose that path and relinquish herself from the feeling it was her obligation to save the world. She still made a difference. She still saved lives and helped others. But, she helped herself too. I admire her for that. But, at the same time, it was clear that she had unfinished business, and Tris felt a deep devotion to completing that work. It was something that Caleb was never going to be capable of doing, so it had to be Tris. It had to. And it required sacrifice. That was her choice.<br />Would it have been nice for us if she had released the serum, AND lived and reunited with Tobias? Yes. But, here is why I think that maybe wasn't the right way to go: Veronica Roth said that she first wrote Divergent from Tobias' perspective. Now I believe that this whole story is really about Tobias, and his growth. Not Tris. I really do. And, now that I see that, I think it was incredibly interesting for Veronica Roth to write the first two books from the perspective of a different character. To experience Four/Tobias through Tris' eyes, and then to experience him through his own voice, was a very complex and deliciously addictive ride. It was so multi-dimensional. She told his story from the way he saw himself, but also from how others saw him. From how someone who chose to love him saw him. In a very twisting way. I can't think of another character sketch (or really a fictional biography) told just quite like this. Tobias was always a broken person, way before he was told he was genetically damaged. I feel like this story is about how Tris facilitated him living through this tragedy and developing into the person he wanted to be. The leader he was meant to become. He had to learn to LET GO. Forgiveness, love, and trust. She completely affected how he interacted with the people and situations around him. This was about him making peace with a traumatic past and becoming an adult. And I never looked at this story like that until the end. I am grateful to Veronica Roth for creating such an interesting tale that was powerfully told. Even if I don't think I can reread and relish it all over and over again like I hoped I could.<br />I actually think my main disappointment with Allegiant was that Uriah's story fell short. I thought he was a very interesting and important character in Divergent. I was upset that his character was cut out of the film. In Insurgent, he was mysterious and delightful, and he helped us bear the phase of Tris, the downer. I loved that he was Divergent, but that he seemed different from the others. He was smart, and thoughtful. I was so sure that he was going to play a key role in Allegiant, and I am let down that it was only his body and memory that played a significant part. I really wanted to see more become of him. But, now that I feel like this was always about Tobias, I guess that really wasn't very important. Can't believe it is over! Worth the read, but keep tissues by your side!

B00BD99JMW|1.0|This is a Spoiler free but Detailed Review of Allegiant.<br /><br />I never was a fan of this trilogy but I wanted to finish it to see how it will all end up and it was poorly wrapped up and a slap in the face to people who are fans.<br /><br />In all honesty this was not good at all. It was Boring. It took way too long to get to the point and to be honest except the very end, far too much was EASY for the characters. Up until the end of the book, there was really no challenge. Anything they did they more or less got away with.<br /><br />1. Plot & Writing<br /><br />This book continues off of the events of Insurgent where Tris has revealed the recording of Edith Prior and the people are now being held against their will by Tobias' Four mother.<br /><br />You would have thought after the events of Book 2 that Book 3 would more or less be adrenaline rushed and non-stop.<br /><br />WRONG....For about the first 20 or so chapters of the book, the book is a bit slow. Yeah there are a few action scenes or intense scenes but for nearly 1/3 of the book it is mainly Tris & Four just sitting around talking.  Talking about their relationship, talking about the plans of what they are going to do, talking about this, talking about that.<br /><br />It was boring to be honest. This is mean to be the final book...you are supposed to go out with a bang and let it be non-stop where the book barely gives you a chance to breathe...Instead, I got bored quite a bit and did the skim reading thing.<br /><br />But then if the first 1/3- 1/2 of the book was slow, then last 1/2 was a bit too rushed. The ending of the book was sort of anti-climatic in a way and I can see why so many people are pissed with the ending. I would be pissed as well if I was a die hard fan. Too much time was spent focused on a less important plot that the biggest shocker of all sort of is just glazed over and I was like...Really? That's how you want to end it.<br /><br />I will say what i enjoyed about this Book compared to the other 3...We get POV from Both Four & Tris. Every other chapter switches between their pov and it really allows you to get into the head of Four(more on this later).<br /><br />2. Characters<br /><br />This book switches between Four & Tris and it does so very well but a lot of time it almost feels like they are the same person. Tris even admits her relationship with FOur is more serious as they don't really have a sense of humor.<br /><br />I've honestly never really cared for their relationship....Tris doesn't have much chemistry with Four and up until Book 3 Four has always seemed one-dimensional to me. I enjoyed getting to know about Four in this book but to be honest...his Parental issues were getting on my nerves. Had this been Book 1 or 2, I wouldn't have minded but this is Book 3 and Four spends most of this book just whining and whining and it's like dude...it's time to get over it.<br /><br />Four also does something completely stupid and ignorant that has horrible consequences and I thought Okay...FINALLY, Four/Tris will become interesting but that only lasts for a couple of chapters and then it is back to \\"Woe is Me\\" Four.<br /><br />Tris was equally boring. She spends the first half of the book sort of doing nothing really but investigating and reading but nothing memorable. The last half of the book, she starts to come alive but for her to be a protagonist and the lead character she was boring. Four, despite all his whining, was far more interesting because at least he had major development.<br /><br />One thing I hate about Tris is that I miss the Tris we sort of saw in Abnegation in Book 1. It's like as soon as she got to Dauntless she became this hard-ass and lost her Abnegation side most of the time. I didn't care about Tris at all in this book.<br /><br />Secondary characters....Eh, they were not that interesting.<br /><br />Peter is here in this book but what was the Point of Peter except a choice he makes at the end? If Peter is going to make a big decision like he did in the end, then give the boy more screentime. He'd be in a scene but he just wouldn't talk. He was literally just there for the book.<br /><br />Christina was likeable and did very well in her supporting role...especially at the end of the book. I enjoyed Christina and I wish she had a little more to do than her sarcasm and being the good friend role.]<br /><br />Caleb had some great development and I did enjoy Tris in her scenes with him. I actually like the character of Caleb because he shows the desperation and flaws of Human nature all in one character.<br /><br />I Also love cara and her quips and sarcasm. She certainly brought humor to the story in her scenes and I am so glad we got Cara in this book.<br />There are a bunch of other secondary characters who are more or less one-dimensional and are merely there to further the plot along.<br /><br />3. Overall<br /><br />This was not a satisfying end to the trilogy.  It was too slow in some parts and the ending not only felt rushed it feels like it sorts of cheats fans of the trilogy.<br /><br />I am no fan but if you are going to go for that ending, give it more of an UMPH and don't make it feel like it is tacked on.<br /><br />I also got a major Hunger games rip-off this book...Especially with Mockingjay. Like there is an event in the book that makes me think of mockingjay and i was like really, that's the best you can come up with?<br /><br />While we discovered the root of the factions, it just feels a bit meh. When the characters discover the truth about the factions and the outside world and what may be out there, i wanted more from them but instead we got more talking and more talking.<br /><br />The biggest thing that annoyed me was Tris/Four in this book. Their relationship troubles are always so petty. They fight over the dumbest of things only to make up a chapter or two later. But they have a legit reason to have an issue and Tris is one of those girls I hate. They credit the fact that they are a strong person to the fact that their man made them feel that way.<br /><br />Really? So had she never met four would Tris always be some shy and quiet abnegation girl? I mean she picked Dauntless before she had even met Four so i would think she'd still be strong and brave even without Four.<br /><br />Ugh.<br /><br />I am so glad this series is over....Definitely a 1.5 out of 5 Stars read.

0316204366|1.0|Back in 1999, Arnold Schwarzenegger, an admitted steroid user, successfully sued a German doctor, Dr. Willi Heepe, for predicting that said bodybuilder would die an early death because of his steroid use. The case Schwarzenegger's lawyers made was that since Dr. Heepe had never examined Schwarzenegger personally, he was in no position to comment on his health.<br /><br />But what does this have to do with Malcolm Gladwell's book, David and Goliath? A lot, actually. In his charismatic narration of the Biblical story of the duel between David and Goliath, Gladwell offers a possible exposé of the unlikely victory that was. He submits, based upon several factors, that Goliath suffered from acromegaly and had a pituitary tumor behind his eyes which David took advantage of in defeating him.<br /><br />Whatever be the conclusions Gladwell derives, and is welcome to derive, from this, there are two critical errors he commits in his analysis of the story that shouldn't be overlooked: One, that Gladwell, despite having no formal training whatsoever in medical science, feels comfortable in making a diagnosis that only the most well trained medical professionals should have the right to diagnose; two, the diagnosis is made based not on any real scientific or medical data but on the grammatical anomalies of a numerously translated text that's not only more than 2000 years old but is also rooted in hearsay (let alone the fact that a rotating sling from a close enough distance can fool anybody with even a 20/20 vision as a multiple).<br /><br />To be fair (or unfair, depending on how you look at it), this idea of diagnosing Goliath isn't Gladwell's. Several unscrupulous charlatans have attempted this malice before, but Gladwell brings it to a wider audience owing to his popularity without showing an ounce of responsibility or ethical conduct. It's easy to divert attention by ascribing importance to the conclusion of the analysis rather than the method of analysis, but diagnosingsomeone without having the authority or sufficient and valid information to do so isn't as benign as it seems and his readers might take it as a sign of encouragement to follow feat. It quickly becomes a social phenomenon, a notorious example of which comes from the `repressed memory' cult of the 90s where callous pop psychology writers (i.e., writers on psychology with no formal training or certification) and overzealous social workers encouraged people to come forward with accusations of molestation against their own parents, despite the undeniable evidence for implanted false memories that can feel as real as repressed memories, which all culminated in the much publicized trial and subsequent exoneration of George Franklin.  There is absolutely no reason for any layman to assume the power to either self-diagnose or diagnose another human being with a life-altering disease, and as little a reason for any formally trained medical professional to do the same without examining the patient or the valid diagnostic data, be the patient alive or long dead.<br /><br />One would expect a writer of Gladwell's reputation to eschew such callousness, but his blatant disregard for social responsibility just to make a point - a point which could easily have been made using modern examples of a seeming underdog defeating an inexorable giant, of which there are plenty - proves the lack of the very thing he is trying to prove he possesses: intellect. If you are looking for a well-deduced synthesis, look elsewhere.
